# Principle for UX Design

> Principle for Mac is a Mac-based UX prototyping tool designed to bring your design ideas to life. In this course, join instructor Tom Green as he shows how to create interactive prototypes for smartphones, tablets, and desktop computers with Principle. The techniques go beyond static wireframes or blueprints; with Principle, your prototypes become more dynamic, with motion and interactivity. Along

> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376) | 3h 13m | 11K learners
> [Jump to Path Context ↓](#path-context)

#### What is Principle?
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/what-is-principle?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/what-is-principle?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Tom] Prototyping interactions, motion, and component functionality is easy with Principle.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/what-is-principle?u=76281980&t=7)** It allows UI designers, motion designers, and interaction designers to communicate their intent by creating artboards that have motion, interactivity, and even transitions between them.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/what-is-principle?u=76281980&t=21)** In this course, we will start by getting familiarized with the Principle interface along with its timeline and drivers.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/what-is-principle?u=76281980&t=28)** We'll also look at how assets are created and added to Principle, how cropping and masking works, and we'll finish with some common prototyping tasks.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/what-is-principle?u=76281980&t=37)** So let's increase our UX design skills, bring our ideas to life, and start prototyping with Principle.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** interface (1), let (1)
> **Speakers:** - [tom] (1)

#### What you should know
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/what-you-should-know?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] This course is aimed at UX design and motion design pros looking for an easy to use tool that allows them to iterate specific interactions and motion designs, which can subsequently be implemented in a prototyping application, such as Figma.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=19)** Now, before we get started, it is important to note that Principle is a Macintosh only application, and unlike other prototyping tools, such as Figma and Adobe XD, Principle does not contain any sort of code introspection feature.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=38)** Since the previous version of Principle, there have been some exciting new additions to the application, including the ability to import your Figma files into Principle, the ability to share your prototypes to the web and a new export menu.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=54)** All of these will be covered in this course.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=57)** Shall we begin?

#### Review of the Principle interface
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Before we get into the actual process of designing and creating prototypes in Principle, I think it's important for you to understand the Principle interface.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=9)** To get yourself started, open the interface file found in your chapter download.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=14)** When it opens, you're looking at a typical Principle project.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=19)** Over here on the left is the properties inspector.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=24)** This is where you'll be doing most of your work.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=26)** Properties are context sensitive, so if you come over here and click on this button, you'll see all the properties for that button light up.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=37)** On top of that, if I move it, you will get these guides that show you placement.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=46)** So this is placed in the middle of the board.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=50)** Now the other thing you can do is you can use the alignment tools up here, flush left, align center, align right, right top edges, center, and of course the bottom edges.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=63)** So you can align multiple objects when you've got them on the artboard.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=69)** You can change the selection's X and Y location by selecting the value.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=76)** And I'll just move it over to say, 200 on the x axis.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=81)** Well, that's 2000, so I'm going to back that up and make it 200.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=87)** And you can see it's shifted over.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=89)** Now another thing you can do is, if you want, notice when I place the cursor over the x here and I scrub, I can move the position using the mouse.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=105)** These are called scrubby numbers and they all apply for everything.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=110)** So if I want to change the opacity, I can do it.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=116)** You can also choose fill colors by clicking this color chip here and this will open the color panel so you can pick a color, and the colors are RGBA, so you can choose a color and then an alpha value, which is going to be very useful later on.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=133)** Now another thing I can do is if I insert a rectangle and I click on this little icon, I can actually choose to fill a shape with an image or a video or some other media.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=148)** These little pop-downs here, I'm just going to select this.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=153)** And these little pop downs here are events.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=155)** So an object can be static, you can drag it, it can be scrolled, or it can be paginated.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=162)** And we'll get into that one later on in this course.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=166)** Clip sub layers is how you actually crop an image.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=171)** And touchable, when selected, allows the artboard to show up on a smartphone, and the actual element that is touchable can be touched rather than clicked.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=184)** Now down here are the artboards, and you notice if I open up the home artboard, they're composed of layers.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=194)** The folders, such as hamburger, indicate groups, and the layers all sit on top of each other.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=201)** So the next image group that I've got selected right here is in its own layer and is composed of a red circle and an arrow.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=214)** Over here is the canvas.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=218)** And you'll notice we've got two artboards on this canvas.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=222)** And if you want to select an artboard you just click on it and it becomes highlighted with green.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=229)** Now this little panel over here is the preview.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=233)** The preview is pretty interesting because it lets you test as you go along.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=237)** So if I come back to the home and I click on this arrow here, I can see that I can go back and forth in the preview.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=250)** And you'll notice that as I roll over the preview panel the cursor changes from an arrow to a circle which is basically indicating where the cursor is or where a finger might be located on a device.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=264)** Now some of you may find this panel to be a bit intrusive and quite frankly, I do as well, but there's a way of hiding it.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=272)** It's up here in the menus.
>
> **[4:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=275)** And if you go to the view menu, you've got two choices, picture in picture and side by side.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=282)** And they manage the preview panel.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=284)** So if I have picture in picture, this is what you see.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=288)** And if I got side by side, you'll notice it goes onto its own screen.
>
> **[4:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=293)** And if you've got two screens, you can just move it over.
>
> **[4:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=296)** The other thing I can do with this panel is shrink it or even minimize it.
>
> **[5:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=305)** So how you want to deal with it is totally up to you.
>
> **[5:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/review-of-the-principle-interface?u=76281980&t=311)** There is a lot more around the interface to cover and we'll pick that up in the next exercise.

#### Tools and menus
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/tools-and-menus?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/tools-and-menus?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] We continue our interface tour by looking at a few more important tools and menu items that you will find extremely useful.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/tools-and-menus?u=76281980&t=10)** If you want to follow along, open the tools and menus file found in your chapter download.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/tools-and-menus?u=76281980&t=16)** At the top of the canvas right here are a number of tools.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/tools-and-menus?u=76281980&t=21)** This insert popped down is where you can add rectangle circles, rounded rectangles, text, and even art boards.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/tools-and-menus?u=76281980&t=30)** Import lets you bring in Sketch or Figma documents.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/tools-and-menus?u=76281980&t=35)** That's about it.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/tools-and-menus?u=76281980&t=37)** Going to close that.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/tools-and-menus?u=76281980&t=39)** In fact, there are other ways of bringing in content and we'll get into that later on in this chapter.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/tools-and-menus?u=76281980&t=45)** Next to the import is create component.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/tools-and-menus?u=76281980&t=49)** This is a tool that lets you turn such items as buttons and switches and things like that into components and reuse them throughout the project.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/tools-and-menus?u=76281980&t=59)** Over here are two of the more important tools of Principle, drivers and animate.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/tools-and-menus?u=76281980&t=68)** They are distinctly different.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/tools-and-menus?u=76281980&t=71)** Drivers animate objects based on changes between properties rather than changes over time or between art boards.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/tools-and-menus?u=76281980&t=83)** Animate pops open a timeline and those arrows that you see up here, let me just scroll down, indicate the direction that we're animating.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/tools-and-menus?u=76281980&t=95)** So we're going from the home to the main, that's what the point.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/tools-and-menus?u=76281980&t=100)** And then there's a back tap.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/tools-and-menus?u=76281980&t=102)** So you find the event and where it goes.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/tools-and-menus?u=76281980&t=106)** And then these are actually the timing and key frames for the animations and we'll get into that later on in this course 'cause there's a lot you can do with these.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/tools-and-menus?u=76281980&t=118)** To dismiss the timeline, all you have to do is click it.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/tools-and-menus?u=76281980&t=122)** The group button serves two purposes.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/tools-and-menus?u=76281980&t=125)** Obviously it groups items and you can also use Command G to group items as well.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/tools-and-menus?u=76281980&t=131)** But it also serves as a crop tool.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/tools-and-menus?u=76281980&t=134)** For instance, an image that is grouped, just the image itself that is in a group can be cropped by selecting clip sub layers over here.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/tools-and-menus?u=76281980&t=145)** Forward and backward really don't do exactly what you think they may do.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/tools-and-menus?u=76281980&t=151)** They move selected items up or down in the layer stack on the properties panel, which is a lot easier than selecting bring to front and send to back in the arrange menu.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/tools-and-menus?u=76281980&t=163)** The share to web tool allows you to share the project to the web.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/tools-and-menus?u=76281980&t=167)** Now what will happen here is the project will be uploaded as a website to a principal server and you can share the link with everybody.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/tools-and-menus?u=76281980&t=177)** Export, if I select it, you get all kinds of choices.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/tools-and-menus?u=76281980&t=182)** You can share a version to the web.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/tools-and-menus?u=76281980&t=185)** You can export a local HTML file and don't get too excited about that.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/tools-and-menus?u=76281980&t=189)** And you can also mirror to iOS.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/tools-and-menus?u=76281980&t=193)** Now, mirror to iOS is rather interesting because it's a fundamental change in Principle.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/tools-and-menus?u=76281980&t=200)** In previous versions of Principle, there was a button up there for Principle Mirror, that was the name of the button, which connected to the Principle Mirror app which is available in the Apple App Store.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/tools-and-menus?u=76281980&t=213)** What this did was to let you connect to your iPhone and preview the project.
>

> **[5:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/add-content-to-an-artboard?u=76281980&t=311)** And you'll notice there are two files in here, Badge.png and Badge 3X.
>
> **[5:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/add-content-to-an-artboard?u=76281980&t=316)** And if I drag in the Batch.png file, it comes in rather huge, and I want to explain why.
>
> **[5:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/add-content-to-an-artboard?u=76281980&t=328)** If I come over here, the art board I am using is actually an iPhone 14 art board which uses a retina screen.
>
> **[5:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/add-content-to-an-artboard?u=76281980&t=338)** You'll notice that inside the folder is also a 3X version of the badge And that is the size for an iPhone 14 screen, which is called a scaling factor, and with an image with a scaling factor applied to it, and this one came from Photoshop and I drop it in.
>
> **[6:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/add-content-to-an-artboard?u=76281980&t=364)** Notice the difference.
>
> **[6:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/add-content-to-an-artboard?u=76281980&t=366)** So if you're working with specific devices, make sure that you have the scaling factors.
>
> **[6:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/add-content-to-an-artboard?u=76281980&t=374)** So there you have it, adding content to an art board.
>
> **[6:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/add-content-to-an-artboard?u=76281980&t=378)** You can drag and drop.

> **[6:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/add-content-to-an-artboard?u=76281980&t=380)** You can copy and paste from Illustrator and Sketch.

> **[6:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/add-content-to-an-artboard?u=76281980&t=391)** Or you can also import an entire Sketch art board and go to work from that.

>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/test-and-share-your-work?u=76281980&t=104)** This is a Mac only feature of course, but if you go to file, export.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/test-and-share-your-work?u=76281980&t=112)** Okay, I'm going to get out of preview.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/test-and-share-your-work?u=76281980&t=114)** File, export, Mac app, and select it.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/test-and-share-your-work?u=76281980&t=121)** So what happens here is it creates a little executable that can be opened on any desktop Mac.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/test-and-share-your-work?u=76281980&t=130)** You're going to make a choice here.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/test-and-share-your-work?u=76281980&t=131)** Do you share a version link, a HTML file, or the app?
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/test-and-share-your-work?u=76281980&t=135)** Well, you're going to export the Mac app.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/test-and-share-your-work?u=76281980&t=138)** And you can see, there it is right there, and that's the executable and it can be opened on any desktop Macintosh on the planet.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/test-and-share-your-work?u=76281980&t=146)** So you can send this to people who have a Mac and when they double click it, the project is able to be viewed but they can't make any changes.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/test-and-share-your-work?u=76281980&t=154)** So just to prove to you that it works, there it is.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/test-and-share-your-work?u=76281980&t=164)** There is another way of sharing your work, and again, it is iOS only.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/test-and-share-your-work?u=76281980&t=168)** What you want to do is to attach the PRD files, the actual files that you're creating.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/test-and-share-your-work?u=76281980&t=173)** And when the recipient opens it, he or she will be prompted to open the file.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/test-and-share-your-work?u=76281980&t=178)** All they have to do on their iOS device is to tap on "copy to principle" and the file will open in the principle app on their device.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/test-and-share-your-work?u=76281980&t=188)** If they don't have the app, they will be prompted to install it.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/test-and-share-your-work?u=76281980&t=192)** I'm just going to close this.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/test-and-share-your-work?u=76281980&t=194)** Another very common way to share files is using the web.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/test-and-share-your-work?u=76281980&t=198)** To do this, select "share to web" and what it will do is it will actually create a web version of the project, write all the HTML and put it on a principle server, very much like working with the cloud.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/test-and-share-your-work?u=76281980&t=216)** And then when you can share that link with all the stakeholders or anybody else and gather some feedback.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/test-and-share-your-work?u=76281980&t=225)** Now, there was one other export option here which was local HTML file.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/test-and-share-your-work?u=76281980&t=232)** It will export an HTML file which you can subsequently edit or pass on to your developer.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/test-and-share-your-work?u=76281980&t=241)** Just be aware, the modernizer JavaScript is minified which may or may not drive your developer crazy.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/test-and-share-your-work?u=76281980&t=250)** My advice, check first.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/test-and-share-your-work?u=76281980&t=253)** So there you have it, there are a number of ways of sharing your files and basically letting the world know that the work is well, somewhat done.

### 2. Managing Content in Principle

#### What Principle doesn't do
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/what-principle-doesn-t-do?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/what-principle-doesn-t-do?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] As you have discovered so far, there is a lot you can do with Principle.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/what-principle-doesn-t-do?u=76281980&t=5)** Yet before we get working with the assets that go into the Principle artboards, let's take a moment to keep in mind what Principle can't do.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/what-principle-doesn-t-do?u=76281980&t=13)** This is important.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/what-principle-doesn-t-do?u=76281980&t=14)** This is not to discourage you from attempting to find the edges of Principle's potential but more to get you into the proper mindset when assembling artboards and projects in Principle.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/what-principle-doesn-t-do?u=76281980&t=27)** At its heart, Principle is essentially an assembly application.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/what-principle-doesn-t-do?u=76281980&t=34)** It is where you place assets on an artboard and then determine how the user will move between them.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/what-principle-doesn-t-do?u=76281980&t=42)** As you move through this chapter, this message will come through loud and clear.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/what-principle-doesn-t-do?u=76281980&t=47)** The actual creative work, the creation of the assets is done elsewhere.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/what-principle-doesn-t-do?u=76281980&t=52)** Vectors, for example, are created in Sketch or even in Illustrator.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/what-principle-doesn-t-do?u=76281980&t=59)** Bitmaps are edited and manipulated in Sketch and/or Photoshop.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/what-principle-doesn-t-do?u=76281980&t=64)** Layouts can be created in artboards in Photoshop or in Sketch and brought into Principle in a manner that will render them properly if they are to be tested on Android or iOS devices.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/what-principle-doesn-t-do?u=76281980&t=77)** Principle doesn't contain the graphics features we are all used to, such as grids, rulers and simple effects such as blurs and things like that because that's not its job.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/what-principle-doesn-t-do?u=76281980&t=90)** Principle's job is to give you the ability to demonstrate your intention around how the user navigates between artboards and how objects move from here to there.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/what-principle-doesn-t-do?u=76281980&t=104)** Let me give you an example.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/what-principle-doesn-t-do?u=76281980&t=107)** If I click this button, we are essentially moving from here to there.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/what-principle-doesn-t-do?u=76281980&t=113)** Though I use images and icons throughout this course, Principle hands you the opportunity to create interactive, low fidelity wireframes and the ability to play with motion interactivity, time, and transitions before the actual assets are even created, such as this wireframe right here to test your assumptions around the movement of the icons before the screen is even created.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/what-principle-doesn-t-do?u=76281980&t=142)** So if I select this artboard and I click on this icon, okay, I've got an idea of how these icons move.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/what-principle-doesn-t-do?u=76281980&t=152)** Principle is not a development tool.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/what-principle-doesn-t-do?u=76281980&t=154)** There is no facility to export code.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/what-principle-doesn-t-do?u=76281980&t=157)** If you take a look in the file menu, there's nothing there that allows you to export anything to do with code.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/what-principle-doesn-t-do?u=76281980&t=163)** Where it is invaluable though is through its ability to allow you to work with a developer and to show him or her how the project works through the use of an interactive prototype.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/what-principle-doesn-t-do?u=76281980&t=177)** Though I have pointed out the practical limits of Principle, in actual fact, the only limits you have with this application are the limits you place on your creativity.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/what-principle-doesn-t-do?u=76281980&t=188)** And you can be very, very creative with this application.
>

#### Create assets in Photoshop
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-assets-in-photoshop?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-assets-in-photoshop?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When you are pulling your Principle projects together, you will discover there is a heavy-duty imaging component involved in creating these projects.

> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-assets-in-photoshop?u=76281980&t=9)** And the odds are pretty good.

> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-assets-in-photoshop?u=76281980&t=18)** As such, there are a couple of things you need to know.

>
> **[6:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-assets-in-photoshop?u=76281980&t=390)** Just add the suffix.
>
> **[6:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-assets-in-photoshop?u=76281980&t=392)** That way he or she knows exactly what you're dealing with.
>
> **[6:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-assets-in-photoshop?u=76281980&t=397)** The bottom line here is to deal to Photoshop's strength and I'm just going to bring the Photoshop image in so we can talk about dealing to Photoshop's strength.
>
> **[6:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-assets-in-photoshop?u=76281980&t=408)** You're going to do all of your imaging adjustment and effects you need in Photoshop, but you need to be aware that they'll be flattened regardless of whether you save the file as a PSD or export it out as either a JPEG or a PNG.
>
> **[7:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-assets-in-photoshop?u=76281980&t=422)** Why PNG?
>
> **[7:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-assets-in-photoshop?u=76281980&t=423)** Well, there are going to be times when you're going to need transparency and PNG is the only bitmap format that supports it.
>
> **[7:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-assets-in-photoshop?u=76281980&t=432)** Also, as I pointed out, forget about using Photoshop artboards because those documents will get flattened in Principle as well.
>
> **[7:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-assets-in-photoshop?u=76281980&t=440)** So to review, you can drag and drop or copy and paste layers into Principle straight out of Photoshop but everything comes in flattened.
>
> **[7:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-assets-in-photoshop?u=76281980&t=449)** Instead, to use export as in Photoshop to output to the required device resolution.
>
> **[7:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-assets-in-photoshop?u=76281980&t=455)** You can output either JPEG or PNG images.
>
> **[7:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-assets-in-photoshop?u=76281980&t=458)** And if you need transparency, make sure you've got transparency selected in the PNG output area.

> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-assets-in-sketch?u=76281980&t=258)** You can also export them out, of course.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-assets-in-sketch?u=76281980&t=260)** Then just drag and drop.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-assets-in-sketch?u=76281980&t=262)** But you can also drag and drop layers between Sketch and Principle.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-assets-in-sketch?u=76281980&t=265)** So that's kind of a neat little technique to keep in your back pocket.

#### Import retina images into Principle
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/import-retina-images-into-principle?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/import-retina-images-into-principle?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] If there is one aspect of UX Design that drives designers up one wall, across the ceiling and down the other wall, it has to be matching image pixel density to devices.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/import-retina-images-into-principle?u=76281980&t=12)** As you saw in the Photoshop and sketch exercises, you can add scaling factor suffixes to ensure images appear at the proper size on a particular device.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/import-retina-images-into-principle?u=76281980&t=24)** Now, when it comes to principle, you can take that three x device and drop it onto an art board, like you see here and it's actually going to come in at one X.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/import-retina-images-into-principle?u=76281980&t=34)** But when you're dealing with scaling factors, it gets pretty interesting.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/import-retina-images-into-principle?u=76281980&t=39)** For example, an image that is 100 by 100 pixels in principle if shown on an iPhone 14, will appear to be only 30 by 30 pixels on the device if there is no scaling factor.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/import-retina-images-into-principle?u=76281980&t=55)** So where do you get these things?

>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/import-retina-images-into-principle?u=76281980&t=77)** Android is, well, it's an open source OS and that has kicked off what I call the wild west.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/import-retina-images-into-principle?u=76281980&t=85)** As the device manufacturers are competing on screen resolution among other features.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/import-retina-images-into-principle?u=76281980&t=92)** This explains why the Android scaling factors are one x, one and a half x, two x, three x and four x and if you want to find out which is which, well you can come here to Stack Overflow and it will tell you exactly which is which, what the scaling factor is, the device, the whole nine yards.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/import-retina-images-into-principle?u=76281980&t=115)** You'll also notice LDPI, which is low dots per inch, XHDPI, which is high DPI, mid and then extra high.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/import-retina-images-into-principle?u=76281980&t=128)** So if you're stuck looking for a device, you might want to come here to Stack Overflow and take a look.

>

> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/crop-and-mask?u=76281980&t=197)** And this is, this little drop here is the spread of the blur.
>

>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/crop-and-mask?u=76281980&t=262)** Cropping and masking inside of Principle.

#### Create motion arcs
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-motion-arcs?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-motion-arcs?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] If there is one fundamental rule that applies to motion in the digital studio, it is that items in motion follow an arc.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-motion-arcs?u=76281980&t=9)** And you may have noticed there's no such thing as a motion path tool, or something that allows you to create a path in principle.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-motion-arcs?u=76281980&t=18)** That's the bad news.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-motion-arcs?u=76281980&t=20)** The good news is there is a rather elegant workaround.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-motion-arcs?u=76281980&t=24)** And let's see how that works.
>

> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-scrollable-content?u=76281980&t=78)** So what's going on here is real simple.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-scrollable-content?u=76281980&t=81)** This group is longer than the viewport so all I have to do is grab this little bottom handle here and pull it up into the viewport.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-scrollable-content?u=76281980&t=91)** And if I scroll, you can see, there we are.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-scrollable-content?u=76281980&t=96)** Now, if I want to get over to The Army, I can choose that, give it a tap, and come over here and I'll select back, give it a tap, come back here.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-scrollable-content?u=76281980&t=118)** And I'm going to go side by side so you can see what's going on.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-scrollable-content?u=76281980&t=125)** So there's the scroll, click and drag.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-scrollable-content?u=76281980&t=129)** And if I click on The Army, there I go, and if I want to go back, there I go.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-scrollable-content?u=76281980&t=134)** So there you have it, that's how you can create a scrolling interface.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-scrollable-content?u=76281980&t=140)** So just a quick review, what you do is you select the content you want to scroll, either vertically or horizontally, group it, trim the group, or just move the bottom handle of the group or one of the handles to the right or the left to make the width of the height you wish.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-scrollable-content?u=76281980&t=155)** And then, select your scroll direction, which in this case was vertical, and you are good to go.

#### Overview of Driver channel
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/overview-of-driver-channel?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/overview-of-driver-channel?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] As you have seen, animations are created from property changes between art boards.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/overview-of-driver-channel?u=76281980&t=5)** There's another way of accomplishing this by animating property changes on a single art board.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/overview-of-driver-channel?u=76281980&t=12)** This is accomplished through what Principle calls drivers.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/overview-of-driver-channel?u=76281980&t=16)** Essentially, drivers establish a parent-child relationship.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/overview-of-driver-channel?u=76281980&t=21)** A property change in the parent is reflected in the property change to the child.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/overview-of-driver-channel?u=76281980&t=28)** Where Principle really shines, is that you choose the properties that will change with the child.

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-a-component?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] A common feature of practically every prototyping application known to man, regardless of platform, is components.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-a-component?u=76281980&t=8)** These are reusable UI elements and in many respects are akin to symbols in a lot of interactive emotion tools out there.
>

#### Create a slide-in menu
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-a-slide-in-menu?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-a-slide-in-menu?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] A common design pattern is a slide-in menu.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-a-slide-in-menu?u=76281980&t=4)** In principle, as you saw earlier in this course, they're surprisingly easy to create.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-a-slide-in-menu?u=76281980&t=10)** In this exercise, we're going to do things a whole lot differently.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-a-slide-in-menu?u=76281980&t=15)** So let's get started, and to get yourself started, open the slide-in menu file found in your chapter download.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-a-slide-in-menu?u=76281980&t=22)** As explained earlier in the course, the key to a slide-in menu is letting the menu take focus when it appears.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-a-slide-in-menu?u=76281980&t=29)** By that, what I mean is what the user should be looking at is the menu and everything else either fades or blurs to minimize any distractions that prevent the user from concentrating on the menu.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/create-a-slide-in-menu?u=76281980&t=45)** And you can see, when you open it up, we've kind of got that in play here.

> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/control-audio-volume?u=76281980&t=127)** Don't set the element to zero.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/control-audio-volume?u=76281980&t=131)** Here we go.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/control-audio-volume?u=76281980&t=132)** Now, I got some good news and I got some bad news regarding this technique.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/control-audio-volume?u=76281980&t=136)** The good news is, holy smokes, you can scrub through video.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/control-audio-volume?u=76281980&t=141)** The bad news is you can't do it while it's playing.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/control-audio-volume?u=76281980&t=144)** If you were to set this video to play, you would lose this driver right here.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/control-audio-volume?u=76281980&t=150)** So just keep that in mind.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/control-audio-volume?u=76281980&t=152)** And the solution to that would be to pause the video, scrub to the area you want and then start playing again.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/control-audio-volume?u=76281980&t=160)** All right, so let's now pay attention to the volume and it's essentially the same technique.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/control-audio-volume?u=76281980&t=170)** Let's get the volume driver, set it to Drag and notice you got another timeline here for that driver and what we're going to do is select the thumb, selected the driver, Center X.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/control-audio-volume?u=76281980&t=187)** Bring it across.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/control-audio-volume?u=76281980&t=189)** Yeah, right about there.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/control-audio-volume?u=76281980&t=191)** And we'll just slide the old thumb over.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/control-audio-volume?u=76281980&t=196)** And if we come back, you can see it moves.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/control-audio-volume?u=76281980&t=199)** Okay, so let's turn off the visibility of this one by knocking back the opacity to zero.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/control-audio-volume?u=76281980&t=208)** Okay, so now what we're going to do is we're going to control the volume of the traffic video and again, if I select the traffic video and I come in here, you notice the volume is zero.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/control-audio-volume?u=76281980&t=223)** So there's a Volume slider just like there's a Time slider.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/control-audio-volume?u=76281980&t=227)** So I'm going to get the pop down, I'm going to select Media Volume and I'm going to come all the way over to this side, open the Media Volume and just slide it right across and let's come back and we'll set this thing to play so you can see the volume at work.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/control-audio-volume?u=76281980&t=257)** Okay, zero volume.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/control-audio-volume?u=76281980&t=267)** Pretty cool, huh?
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/control-audio-volume?u=76281980&t=269)** So there you go.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/control-audio-volume?u=76281980&t=270)** There's how you can scrub video by using the Media Time property right there and adjust the volume of your video by using the Volume property.
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/control-audio-volume?u=76281980&t=285)** And by the way, this also works with audio files that you may drop in, individual MP3 files that you may drop in.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/principle-for-ux-design-22683376/control-audio-volume?u=76281980&t=294)** You can control the volume quite nicely using this property.
